       <a href="#cite_note-lanl-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      as part of oxide compounds such as
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">
       silicon dioxide
      </a>
      and is the most abundant element by mass in the
      <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)#Earth.27s_crust_and_mantle" title="Crust (geology)">
       Earth's crust
      </a>
      . It is also the major component of the world's oceans (88.8% by mass).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_5-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE500-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxygen gas is the second most common component of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">
       Earth's atmosphere
      </a>
      , taking up 20.8% of its volume and 23.1% of its mass (some 10
      <sup>
       15
      </sup>
      tonnes).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_5-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE500-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB298-60">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [d]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Earth is unusual among the planets of the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
       Solar System
      </a>
      in having such a high concentration of oxygen gas in its atmosphere:
      <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
       Mars
      </a>
      (with 0.1%
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      by volume) and
      <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
       Venus
      </a>
      have much less. The
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      surrounding those planets is produced solely by ultraviolet radiation on oxygen-containing molecules such as carbon dioxide.
     </p>
     <p>
      The unusually high concentration of oxygen gas on Earth is the result of the
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_cycle" title="Oxygen cycle">
       oxygen cycle
      </a>
      . This
      <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">
       biogeochemical cycle
      </a>
      describes the movement of oxygen within and between its three main reservoirs on Earth: the atmosphere, the
      <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">
       biosphere
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">
       lithosphere
      </a>
      . The main driving factor of the oxygen cycle is
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
       photosynthesis
      </a>
      , which is responsible for modern Earth's atmosphere. Photosynthesis releases oxygen into the atmosphere, while
      <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">
       respiration
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">
       decay
      </a>
      , and combustion remove it from the atmosphere. In the present equilibrium, production and consumption occur at the same rate of roughly 1/2000th of the entire atmospheric oxygen per year.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png">
        <img alt="World map showing that the sea-surface oxygen is depleted around the equator and increases towards the poles." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2217" data-file-width="3158" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png/220px-WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png/330px-WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png/440px-WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Cold water holds more dissolved
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Free oxygen also occurs in solution in the world's water bodies. The increased solubility of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      at lower temperatures (see
      <a href="#Physical_properties">
       Physical properties
      </a>
      ) has important implications for ocean life, as polar oceans support a much higher density of life due to their higher oxygen content.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">
       Water polluted
      </a>
      with plant nutrients such as
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">
       nitrates
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">
       phosphates
      </a>
      may stimulate growth of algae by a process called
      <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">
       eutrophication
      </a>
      and the decay of these organisms and other biomaterials may reduce the
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      content in eutrophic water bodies. Scientists assess this aspect of water quality by measuring the water's
      <a href="/wiki/Biochemical_oxygen_demand" title="Biochemical oxygen demand">
       biochemical oxygen demand
      </a>
      , or the amount of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      needed to restore it to a normal concentration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">
       Analysis
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png">
        <img alt="Time evolution of oxygen-18 concentration on the scale of 500 million years showing many local peaks." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="726" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png/250px-Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png/375px-Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png/500px-Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        500 million years of
        <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">
         climate change
        </a>
        vs
        <sup>
         18
        </sup>
        O
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">
       Paleoclimatologists
      </a>
      measure the ratio of oxygen-18 and oxygen-16 in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_shell" title="Animal shell">
       shells
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">
       skeletons
      </a>
      of marine organisms to determine the climate millions of years ago (see
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_isotope_ratio_cycle" title="Oxygen isotope ratio cycle">
       oxygen isotope ratio cycle
      </a>
      ).
      <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">
       Seawater
      </a>
      molecules that contain the lighter
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">
       isotope
      </a>
      , oxygen-16, evaporate at a slightly faster rate than water molecules containing the 12% heavier oxygen-18, and this disparity increases at lower temperatures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB304_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB304-64">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During periods of lower global temperatures, snow and rain from that evaporated water tends to be higher in oxygen-16, and the seawater left behind tends to be higher in oxygen-18. Marine organisms then incorporate more oxygen-18 into their skeletons and shells than they would in a warmer climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB304_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB304-64">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Paleoclimatologists also directly measure this ratio in the water molecules of
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">
       ice core
      </a>
      samples as old as hundreds of thousands of years.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_solar_terrestrial_planets" title="Geology of solar terrestrial planets">
       Planetary geologists
      </a>
      have measured the relative quantities of oxygen isotopes in samples from the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
       Moon
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
       Mars
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">
       meteorites
      </a>
      , but were long unable to obtain reference values for the isotope ratios in the
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
      , believed to be the same as those of the
      <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">
       primordial solar nebula
      </a>
      . Analysis of a
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
       silicon
      </a>
      wafer exposed to the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
       solar wind
      </a>
      in space and returned by the crashed
      <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">
       Genesis spacecraft
      </a>
      has shown that the Sun has a higher proportion of oxygen-16 than does the Earth. The measurement implies that an unknown process depleted oxygen-16 from the Sun's
      <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">
       disk of protoplanetary material
      </a>
      prior to the coalescence of dust grains that formed the Earth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen presents two spectrophotometric
      <a href="/wiki/Absorption_band" title="Absorption band">
       absorption bands
      </a>
      peaking at the wavelengths 687 and 760
      <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">
       nm
      </a>
      . Some
      <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">
       remote sensing
      </a>
      scientists have proposed using the measurement of the radiance coming from vegetation canopies in those bands to characterize plant health status from a
      <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">
       satellite
      </a>
      platform.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This approach exploits the fact that in those bands it is possible to discriminate the vegetation's
      <a href="/wiki/Reflectance" title="Reflectance">
       reflectance
      </a>
      from its
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">
       fluorescence
      </a>
      , which is much weaker. The measurement is technically difficult owing to the low
      <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">
       signal-to-noise ratio
      </a>
      and the physical structure of vegetation; but it has been proposed as a possible method of monitoring the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
       carbon cycle
      </a>
      from satellites on a global scale.
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role_of_O2">
       Biological role of O
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Dioxygen_in_biological_reactions" title="Dioxygen in biological reactions">
       Dioxygen in biological reactions
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Photosynthesis_and_respiration">
       Photosynthesis and respiration
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg">
        <img alt="A diagram of photosynthesis processes, including income of water and carbon dioxide, illumination and release of oxygen. Reactions produce ATP and NADPH in a Calvin cycle with a sugar as a by product." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1027" data-file-width="830" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/220px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/330px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/440px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Photosynthesis splits water to liberate
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        and fixes
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         CO
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        into sugar in what is called a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">
         Calvin cycle
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In nature, free oxygen is produced by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">
       light-driven splitting
      </a>
      of water during oxygenic
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
       photosynthesis
      </a>
      . According to some estimates,
      <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">
       green algae
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">
       cyanobacteria
      </a>
      in marine environments provide about 70% of the free oxygen produced on Earth, and the rest is produced by terrestrial plants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other estimates of the oceanic contribution to atmospheric oxygen are higher, while some estimates are lower, suggesting oceans produce ~45% of Earth's atmospheric oxygen each year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A simplified overall formula for photosynthesis is:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         6 CO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         + 6
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          H
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
          O
         </span>
         +
         <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">
          photons
         </a>
         →
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          C
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           6
          </span>
          H
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           12
          </span>
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           6
          </span>
         </span>
         + 6
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
         </span>
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      or simply
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
          carbon dioxide
         </a>
         + water + sunlight →
         <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">
          glucose
         </a>
         + dioxygen
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Photolytic
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolution" title="Oxygen evolution">
       oxygen evolution
      </a>
      occurs in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thylakoid_membrane" title="Thylakoid membrane">
       thylakoid membranes
      </a>
      of photosynthetic organisms and requires the energy of four
      <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">
       photons
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [e]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many steps are involved, but the result is the formation of a
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       proton
      </a>
      gradient across the thylakoid membrane, which is used to synthesize
      <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">
       adenosine triphosphate
      </a>
      (ATP) via
      <a href="/wiki/Photophosphorylation" title="Photophosphorylation">
       photophosphorylation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raven-71">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      remaining (after production of the water molecule) is released into the atmosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [f]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen is used in
      <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">
       mitochondria
      </a>
      to generate ATP during
      <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">
       oxidative phosphorylation
      </a>
      . The reaction for aerobic respiration is essentially the reverse of photosynthesis and is simplified as:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          C
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           6
          </span>
          H
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           12
          </span>
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           6
          </span>
         </span>
         + 6
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
         </span>
         → 6 CO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         + 6
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          H
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
          O
         </span>
         + 2880 kJ·mol
         <sup>
          −1
         </sup>
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">
       vertebrates
      </a>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">
       diffuses
      </a>
      through membranes in the lungs and into
      <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">
       red blood cells
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">
       Hemoglobin
      </a>
      binds
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      , changing color from bluish red to bright red
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuideElem48_40-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-GuideElem48-40">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      is released from another part of hemoglobin through the
      <a href="/wiki/Bohr_effect" title="Bohr effect">
       Bohr effect
      </a>
      ). Other animals use
      <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">
       hemocyanin
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">
       molluscs
      </a>
      and some
      <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">
       arthropods
      </a>
      ) or
      <a href="/wiki/Hemerythrin" title="Hemerythrin">
       hemerythrin
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">
       spiders
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">
       lobsters
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB298-60">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A liter of blood can dissolve 200 cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_60-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB298-60">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Until the discovery of
      <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">
       anaerobic
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metazoa" title="Metazoa">
       metazoa
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20370908_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20370908-73">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      oxygen was thought to be a requirement for all complex life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">
       Reactive oxygen species
      </a>
      , such as
      <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">
       superoxide
      </a>
      ion (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        −
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">
       hydrogen peroxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       H
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ), are reactive by-products of oxygen use in organisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_60-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB298-60">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parts of the
      <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">
       immune system
      </a>
      of higher organisms create peroxide, superoxide, and singlet oxygen to destroy invading microbes. Reactive oxygen species also play an important role in the
      <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitive_response" title="Hypersensitive response">
       hypersensitive response
      </a>
      of plants against pathogen attack.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_71-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raven-71">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxygen is damaging to
      <a href="/wiki/Obligate_anaerobe" title="Obligate anaerobe">
       obligately anaerobic organisms
      </a>
      , which were the dominant form of
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">
       early life
      </a>
      on Earth until
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      began to accumulate in the
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
       atmosphere
      </a>
      about 2.5 billion years ago during the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">
       Great Oxygenation Event
      </a>
      , about a billion years after the first appearance of these organisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-76">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An adult human at rest
      <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">
       inhales
      </a>
      1.8 to 2.4 grams of oxygen per minute.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This amounts to more than 6 billion tonnes of oxygen inhaled by humanity per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [g]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Living_organisms">
       Living organisms
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <span id="partial_pressure">
      </span>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="float:right">
      <caption>
       <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">
        Partial pressures
       </a>
       of oxygen in the human body (PO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       )
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        Unit
       </th>
       <th>
        Alveolar
        <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_gas_pressures" title="Pulmonary gas pressures">
         pulmonary
         <br/>
         gas pressures
        </a>
       </th>
       <th>
        <a href="/wiki/Arterial_blood_gas#oxygen" title="Arterial blood gas">
         Arterial blood oxygen
        </a>
       </th>
       <th>
        <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">
         Venous
        </a>
        blood gas
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KPa" title="KPa">
         kPa
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        14.2
       </td>
       <td>
        11
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmHg_79-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-mmHg-79">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        -13
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmHg_79-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-mmHg-79">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td>
        4.0
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmHg_79-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-mmHg-79">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        -5.3
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmHg_79-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-mmHg-79">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MmHg" title="MmHg">
         mmHg
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        107
       </td>
       <td>
        75
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southwest_80-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-southwest-80">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        -100
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southwest_80-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-southwest-80">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td>
        30
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brookside_81-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-brookside-81">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
        -40
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brookside_81-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-brookside-81">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The free oxygen
      <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">
       partial pressure
      </a>
      in the body of a living vertebrate organism is highest in the
      <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">
       respiratory system
      </a>
      , and decreases along any
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_system" title="Arterial system">
       arterial system
      </a>
      , peripheral tissues, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venous_system" title="Venous system">
       venous system
      </a>
      , respectively. Partial pressure is the pressure that oxygen would have if it alone occupied the volume.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Build-up_in_the_atmosphere">
       Build-up in the atmosphere
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_oxygen" title="Geological history of oxygen">
       Geological history of oxygen
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxygenation-atm.svg">
        <img alt="A graph showing time evolution of oxygen pressure on Earth; the pressure increases from zero to 0.2 atmospheres." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="840" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oxygenation-atm.svg/300px-Oxygenation-atm.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oxygenation-atm.svg/450px-Oxygenation-atm.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oxygenation-atm.svg/600px-Oxygenation-atm.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxygenation-atm.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        build-up in Earth's atmosphere: 1) no
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        produced; 2)
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        produced, but absorbed in oceans &amp; seabed rock; 3)
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        starts to gas out of the oceans, but is absorbed by land surfaces and formation of ozone layer; 4–5)
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        sinks filled and the gas accumulates
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Free oxygen gas was almost nonexistent in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">
       Earth's atmosphere
      </a>
      before photosynthetic
      <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">
       archaea
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      evolved, probably about 3.5 billion years ago. Free oxygen first appeared in significant quantities during the
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoproterozoic" title="Paleoproterozoic">
       Paleoproterozoic
      </a>
      eon (between 3.0 and 2.3 billion years ago).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowe2013_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Crowe2013-83">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the first billion years, any free oxygen produced by these organisms combined with dissolved
      <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
       iron
      </a>
      in the oceans to form
      <a href="/wiki/Banded_iron_formation" title="Banded iron formation">
       banded iron formations
      </a>
      . When such oxygen sinks became saturated, free oxygen began to
      <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">
       outgas
      </a>
      from the oceans 3–2.7 billion years ago, reaching 10% of its present level around 1.7 billion years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowe2013_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Crowe2013-83">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Campbell-84">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The presence of large amounts of dissolved and free oxygen in the oceans and atmosphere may have driven most of the extant
      <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">
       anaerobic organisms
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">
       extinction
      </a>
      during the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">
       Great Oxygenation Event
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       oxygen catastrophe
      </i>
      ) about 2.4 billion years ago.
      <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">
       Cellular respiration
      </a>
      using
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      enables
      <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">
       aerobic organisms
      </a>
      to produce much more
      <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">
       ATP
      </a>
      than
      <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">
       anaerobic organisms
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Freeman-85">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cellular respiration of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      occurs in all
      <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">
       eukaryotes
      </a>
      , including all complex multicellular organisms such as plants and animals.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the beginning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">
       Cambrian
      </a>
      period 540 million years ago, atmospheric
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      levels have fluctuated between 15% and 30% by volume.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geologic_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-geologic-86">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Towards the end of the
      <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">
       Carboniferous
      </a>
      period (about 300 million years ago) atmospheric
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      levels reached a maximum of 35% by volume,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geologic_86-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-geologic-86">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which may have contributed to the large size of insects and amphibians at this time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2009_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2009-87">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Variations of oxygen shaped the climates of the past. When oxygen declined, atmospheric density dropped and this in turn increased surface evaporation, and led to precipitation increases and warmer temperatures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the current rate of photosynthesis it would take about 2,000 years to regenerate the entire
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      in the present atmosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_production">
       Industrial production
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg">
        <img alt="A drawing of three vertical pipes connected at the bottom and filled with oxygen (left pipe), water (middle) and hydrogen (right). Anode and cathode electrodes are inserted into the left and right pipes and externally connected to a battery." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="209" height="342" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg/220px-Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg/330px-Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg/440px-Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Hofmann_voltameter" title="Hofmann voltameter">
         Hofmann electrolysis apparatus
        </a>
        used in electrolysis of water.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Air_separation" title="Air separation">
       Air separation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolution" title="Oxygen evolution">
       Oxygen evolution
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">
       fractional distillation
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      One hundred million tonnes of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      are extracted from air for industrial uses annually by two primary methods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_16-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB300-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most common method is
      <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">
       fractional distillation
      </a>
      of liquefied air, with
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">
       distilling
      </a>
      as a vapor while
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      is left as a liquid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_16-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB300-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The other primary method of producing
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      is passing a stream of clean, dry air through one bed of a pair of identical
      <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">
       zeolite
      </a>
      molecular sieves, which absorbs the nitrogen and delivers a gas stream that is 90% to 93%
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_16-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB300-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Simultaneously, nitrogen gas is released from the other nitrogen-saturated zeolite bed, by reducing the chamber operating pressure and diverting part of the oxygen gas from the producer bed through it, in the reverse direction of flow. After a set cycle time the operation of the two beds is interchanged, thereby allowing for a continuous supply of gaseous oxygen to be pumped through a pipeline. This is known as
      <a href="/wiki/Pressure_swing_adsorption" title="Pressure swing adsorption">
       pressure swing adsorption
      </a>
      . Oxygen gas is increasingly obtained by these non-
      <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">
       cryogenic
      </a>
      technologies (see also the related
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_swing_adsorption" title="Vacuum swing adsorption">
       vacuum swing adsorption
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen gas can also be produced through
      <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">
       electrolysis of water
      </a>
      into molecular oxygen and hydrogen. DC electricity must be used: if AC is used, the gases in each limb consist of hydrogen and oxygen in the explosive ratio 2:1. Contrary to popular belief, the 2:1 ratio observed in the DC electrolysis of acidified water does not prove that the empirical formula of water is H
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O unless certain assumptions are made about the molecular formulae of hydrogen and oxygen themselves. A similar method is the electrocatalytic
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      evolution from oxides and
      <a href="/wiki/Oxoacid" title="Oxoacid">
       oxoacids
      </a>
      . Chemical catalysts can be used as well, such as in
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator" title="Chemical oxygen generator">
       chemical oxygen generators
      </a>
      or oxygen candles that are used as part of the life-support equipment on submarines, and are still part of standard equipment on commercial airliners in case of depressurization emergencies. Another air separation method is forcing air to dissolve through
      <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">
       ceramic
      </a>
      membranes based on
      <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide">
       zirconium dioxide
      </a>
      by either high pressure or an electric current, to produce nearly pure
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      gas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In large quantities, the price of liquid oxygen in 2001 was approximately $0.21/kg.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since the primary cost of production is the energy cost of liquefying the air, the production cost will change as energy cost varies.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">
       Storage
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_storage" title="Oxygen storage">
       Oxygen storage
      </a>
      methods include high pressure
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_tank" title="Oxygen tank">
       oxygen tanks
      </a>
      , cryogenics and chemical compounds. For reasons of economy, oxygen is often transported in bulk as a liquid in specially insulated tankers, since one
      <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">
       liter
      </a>
      of liquefied oxygen is equivalent to 840 liters of gaseous oxygen at atmospheric pressure and 20 °C (68 °F).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_16-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB300-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such tankers are used to refill bulk liquid oxygen storage containers, which stand outside hospitals and other institutions that need large volumes of pure oxygen gas. Liquid oxygen is passed through
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">
       heat exchangers
      </a>
      , which convert the cryogenic liquid into gas before it enters the building. Oxygen is also stored and shipped in smaller cylinders containing the compressed gas; a form that is useful in certain portable medical applications and
      <a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_welding_and_cutting" title="Oxy-fuel welding and cutting">
       oxy-fuel welding and cutting
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_16-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB300-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">
       Applications
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">
       Breathing gas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">
       Redox
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">
       Combustion
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">
       Medical
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg">
        <img alt="A gray device with a label DeVILBISS LT4000 and some text on the front panel. A green plastic pipe is running from the device." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1224" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg/170px-Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg/255px-Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg/340px-Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An
        <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_concentrator" title="Oxygen concentrator">
         oxygen concentrator
        </a>
        in an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">
         emphysema
        </a>
        patient's house
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_therapy" title="Oxygen therapy">
       Oxygen therapy
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Uptake of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      from the air is the essential purpose of
      <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">
       respiration
      </a>
      , so oxygen supplementation is used in
      <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">
       medicine
      </a>
      . Treatment not only increases oxygen levels in the patient's blood, but has the secondary effect of decreasing resistance to blood flow in many types of diseased lungs, easing work load on the heart.
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_therapy" title="Oxygen therapy">
       Oxygen therapy
      </a>
      is used to treat
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">
       emphysema
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">
       pneumonia
      </a>
      , some heart disorders (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" title="Congestive heart failure">
       congestive heart failure
      </a>
      ), some disorders that cause increased
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery_pressure" title="Pulmonary artery pressure">
       pulmonary artery pressure
      </a>
      , and any
      <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">
       disease
      </a>
      that impairs the body's ability to take up and use gaseous oxygen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE510_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE510-92">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Treatments are flexible enough to be used in hospitals, the patient's home, or increasingly by portable devices.
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_tent" title="Oxygen tent">
       Oxygen tents
      </a>
      were once commonly used in oxygen supplementation, but have since been replaced mostly by the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_mask" title="Oxygen mask">
       oxygen masks
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Nasal_cannula" title="Nasal cannula">
       nasal cannulas
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18540928_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18540928-93">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_medicine" title="Hyperbaric medicine">
       Hyperbaric
      </a>
      (high-pressure) medicine uses special
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_oxygen_chamber" title="Hyperbaric oxygen chamber">
       oxygen chambers
      </a>
      to increase the
      <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">
       partial pressure
      </a>
      of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      around the patient and, when needed, the medical staff.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8931286_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid8931286-94">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">
       Carbon monoxide poisoning
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_gangrene" title="Gas gangrene">
       gas gangrene
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">
       decompression sickness
      </a>
      (the 'bends') are sometimes addressed with this therapy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Increased
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      concentration in the lungs helps to displace
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
       carbon monoxide
      </a>
      from the heme group of
      <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">
       hemoglobin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15233173_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15233173-97">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxygen gas is poisonous to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_bacteria" title="Anaerobic bacteria">
       anaerobic bacteria
      </a>
      that cause gas gangrene, so increasing its partial pressure helps kill them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Decompression sickness occurs in divers who decompress too quickly after a dive, resulting in bubbles of inert gas, mostly nitrogen and helium, forming in the blood. Increasing the pressure of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      as soon as possible helps to redissolve the bubbles back into the blood so that these excess gasses can be exhaled naturally through the lungs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE510_92-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE510-92">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen is also used medically for patients who require
      <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">
       mechanical ventilation
      </a>
      , often at concentrations above the 21% found in ambient air.
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life_support_and_recreational_use">
       Life support and recreational use
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3011" data-file-width="3000" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg/220px-Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg/330px-Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg/440px-Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wisoff_on_the_Arm_-_GPN-2000-001069.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Low pressure pure
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        is used in
        <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">
         space suits
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An application of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      as a low-pressure
      <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">
       breathing gas
      </a>
      is in modern
      <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">
       space suits
      </a>
      , which surround their occupant's body with pressurized air. These devices use nearly pure oxygen at about one third normal pressure, resulting in a normal blood partial pressure of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11541018_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11541018-102">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2730484_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2730484-103">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This trade-off of higher oxygen concentration for lower pressure is needed to maintain suit flexibility.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">
       Scuba divers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">
       submariners
      </a>
      also rely on artificially delivered
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      , but most often use normal pressure, and/or mixtures of oxygen and air. Pure or nearly pure
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      use in diving at higher-than-sea-level pressures is usually limited to rebreather, decompression, or emergency treatment use at relatively shallow depths (~6 meters depth, or less).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acott_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Acott-104">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longphre_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Longphre-105">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deeper diving requires significant dilution of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      with other gases, such as nitrogen or helium, to prevent
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_toxicity" title="Oxygen toxicity">
       oxygen toxicity
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acott_104-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Acott-104">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      People who climb mountains or fly in non-pressurized
      <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">
       fixed-wing aircraft
      </a>
      sometimes have supplemental
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      supplies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        [h]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pressurized commercial airplanes have an emergency supply of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      automatically supplied to the passengers in case of cabin depressurization. Sudden cabin pressure loss activates
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator" title="Chemical oxygen generator">
       chemical oxygen generators
      </a>
      above each seat, causing
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_mask" title="Oxygen mask">
       oxygen masks
      </a>
      to drop. Pulling on the masks "to start the flow of oxygen" as cabin safety instructions dictate, forces iron filings into the
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chlorate" title="Sodium chlorate">
       sodium chlorate
      </a>
      inside the canister.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A steady stream of oxygen gas is then produced by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">
       exothermic
      </a>
      reaction.
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen, as a supposed mild
      <a href="/wiki/Euphoria" title="Euphoria">
       euphoric
      </a>
      , has a history of recreational use in
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_bar" title="Oxygen bar">
       oxygen bars
      </a>
      and in
      <a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">
       sports
      </a>
      . Oxygen bars are establishments found in Japan,
      <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
       California
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Valley" title="Las Vegas Valley">
       Las Vegas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">
       Nevada
      </a>
      since the late 1990s that offer higher than normal
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      exposure for a fee.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA-O2Bars_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FDA-O2Bars-107">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Professional athletes, especially in
      <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">
       American football
      </a>
      , sometimes go off-field between plays to don oxygen masks to boost performance. The pharmacological effect is doubted; a
      <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">
       placebo
      </a>
      effect is a more likely explanation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA-O2Bars_107-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FDA-O2Bars-107">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Available studies support a performance boost from enriched
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      mixtures only if it is breathed
      <i>
       during
      </i>
      aerobic exercise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other recreational uses that do not involve breathing include
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrotechnic" title="Pyrotechnic">
       pyrotechnic
      </a>
      applications, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Goble" title="George Goble">
       George Goble
      </a>
      's five-second ignition of
      <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">
       barbecue
      </a>
      grills.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">
       Industrial
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clabecq_JPG01.jpg">
        <img alt="An elderly worker in a helmet is facing his side to the viewer in an industrial hall. The hall is dark but is illuminated yellow glowing splashes of a melted substance." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="616" data-file-width="825" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Clabecq_JPG01.jpg/220px-Clabecq_JPG01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Clabecq_JPG01.jpg/330px-Clabecq_JPG01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Clabecq_JPG01.jpg/440px-Clabecq_JPG01.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clabecq_JPG01.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Most commercially produced
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        is used to
        <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">
         smelt
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
         iron
        </a>
        into
        <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">
         steel
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">
       Smelting
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">
       iron ore
      </a>
      into
      <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">
       steel
      </a>
      consumes 55% of commercially produced oxygen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In this process,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      is injected through a high-pressure lance into molten iron, which removes
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
       sulfur
      </a>
      impurities and excess
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      as the respective oxides,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       SO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      . The reactions are
      <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">
       exothermic
      </a>
      , so the temperature increases to 1,700 °
      <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">
       C
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another 25% of commercially produced oxygen is used by the chemical industry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">
       Ethylene
      </a>
      is reacted with
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      to create
      <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">
       ethylene oxide
      </a>
      , which, in turn, is converted into
      <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">
       ethylene glycol
      </a>
      ; the primary feeder material used to manufacture a host of products, including
      <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">
       antifreeze
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">
       polyester
      </a>
      polymers (the precursors of many
      <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">
       plastics
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabric" title="Fabric">
       fabrics
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most of the remaining 20% of commercially produced oxygen is used in medical applications,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_welding" title="Gas welding">
       metal cutting and welding
      </a>
      , as an oxidizer in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" title="Rocket fuel">
       rocket fuel
      </a>
      , and in
      <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">
       water treatment
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_63-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB301-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxygen is used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxyacetylene_welding" title="Oxyacetylene welding">
       oxyacetylene welding
      </a>
      burning
      <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">
       acetylene
      </a>
      with
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      to produce a very hot flame. In this process, metal up to 60 cm (24 in) thick is first heated with a small oxy-acetylene flame and then quickly cut by a large stream of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE508_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE508-110">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">
       Compounds
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_oxygen" title="Compounds of oxygen">
       Compounds of oxygen
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg">
        <img alt="Water flowing from a bottle into a glass." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="404" data-file-width="298" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg/170px-Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg/255px-Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
         Water
        </a>
        (
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         H
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
         O
        </span>
        ) is the most familiar oxygen compound.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">
       oxidation state
      </a>
      of oxygen is −2 in almost all known compounds of oxygen. The oxidation state −1 is found in a few compounds such as
      <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">
       peroxides
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Compounds containing oxygen in other oxidation states are very uncommon: −1/2 (
      <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">
       superoxides
      </a>
      ), −1/3 (
      <a href="/wiki/Ozonide" title="Ozonide">
       ozonides
      </a>
      ), 0 (
      <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_oxygen" title="Allotropes of oxygen">
       elemental
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hypofluorous_acid" title="Hypofluorous acid">
       hypofluorous acid
      </a>
      ), +1/2 (
      <a href="/wiki/Dioxygenyl" title="Dioxygenyl">
       dioxygenyl
      </a>
      ), +1 (
      <a href="/wiki/Dioxygen_difluoride" title="Dioxygen difluoride">
       dioxygen difluoride
      </a>
      ), and +2 (
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_difluoride" title="Oxygen difluoride">
       oxygen difluoride
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_other_inorganic_compounds">
       Oxides and other inorganic compounds
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
       Water
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       H
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
      </span>
      ) is an oxide of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      and the most familiar oxygen compound. Hydrogen atoms are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent_bonding" title="Covalent bonding">
       covalently bonded
      </a>
      to oxygen in a water molecule but also have an additional attraction (about 23.3 kJ·mol
      <sup>
       −1
      </sup>
      per hydrogen atom) to an adjacent oxygen atom in a separate molecule.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">
       hydrogen bonds
      </a>
      between water molecules hold them approximately 15% closer than what would be expected in a simple liquid with just
      <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">
       van der Waals forces
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        [i]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rust_screw.jpg">
        <img alt="A rusty piece of a bolt." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rust_screw.jpg/220px-Rust_screw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rust_screw.jpg/330px-Rust_screw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rust_screw.jpg/440px-Rust_screw.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rust_screw.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Oxides, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">
         iron oxide
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">
         rust
        </a>
        , form when oxygen combines with other elements.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Due to its
      <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">
       electronegativity
      </a>
      , oxygen forms
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">
       chemical bonds
      </a>
      with almost all other elements to give corresponding
      <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">
       oxides
      </a>
      . The surface of most metals, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">
