       aluminium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
       titanium
      </a>
      , are oxidized in the presence of air and become coated with a thin film of oxide that
      <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">
       passivates
      </a>
      the metal and slows further
      <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">
       corrosion
      </a>
      . Many oxides of the
      <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">
       transition metals
      </a>
      are
      <a href="/wiki/Non-stoichiometric_compound" title="Non-stoichiometric compound">
       non-stoichiometric compounds
      </a>
      , with slightly less metal than the
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">
       chemical formula
      </a>
      would show. For example, the mineral
      <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_oxide" title="Iron(II) oxide">
       FeO
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCstite" title="Wüstite">
       wüstite
      </a>
      ) is written as
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Fe
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        1 −
        <i>
         x
        </i>
       </span>
       O
      </span>
      , where
      <i>
       x
      </i>
      is usually around 0.05.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen is present in the atmosphere in trace quantities in the form of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ). The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">
       Earth's crustal
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
       rock
      </a>
      is composed in large part of oxides of
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
       silicon
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">
       silica
      </a>
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      , as found in
      <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">
       granite
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">
       quartz
      </a>
      ), aluminium (
      <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">
       aluminium oxide
      </a>
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Al
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      , in
      <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">
       bauxite
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">
       corundum
      </a>
      ), iron (
      <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">
       iron(III) oxide
      </a>
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Fe
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      , in
      <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">
       hematite
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">
       rust
      </a>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">
       calcium carbonate
      </a>
      (in
      <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">
       limestone
      </a>
      ). The rest of the Earth's crust is also made of oxygen compounds, in particular various complex
      <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
       silicates
      </a>
      (in
      <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">
       silicate minerals
      </a>
      ). The Earth's mantle, of much larger mass than the crust, is largely composed of silicates of magnesium and iron.
     </p>
     <p>
      Water-
      <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">
       soluble
      </a>
      silicates in the form of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Na
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Na
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      , and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Na
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       Si
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        5
       </span>
      </span>
      are used as
      <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">
       detergents
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Adhesive" title="Adhesive">
       adhesives
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE507_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE507-116">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen also acts as a
      <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">
       ligand
      </a>
      for transition metals, forming
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transition_metal_dioxygen_complexes" title="Transition metal dioxygen complexes">
       transition metal dioxygen complexes
      </a>
      , which feature metal–
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      . This class of compounds includes the
      <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">
       heme
      </a>
      proteins
      <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">
       hemoglobin
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">
       myoglobin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An exotic and unusual reaction occurs with
      <a href="/wiki/Platinum_hexafluoride" title="Platinum hexafluoride">
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        PtF
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         6
        </span>
       </span>
      </a>
      , which oxidizes oxygen to give O
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      <sup>
       +
      </sup>
      PtF
      <sub>
       6
      </sub>
      <sup>
       −
      </sup>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE505_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE505-118">
        [109]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_compounds_and_biomolecules">
       Organic compounds and biomolecules
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetone-3D-vdW.png">
        <img alt="A ball structure of a molecule. Its backbone is a zig-zag chain of three carbon atoms connected in the center to an oxygen atom and on the end to 6 hydrogens." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="1100" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Acetone-3D-vdW.png/220px-Acetone-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Acetone-3D-vdW.png/330px-Acetone-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Acetone-3D-vdW.png/440px-Acetone-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acetone-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">
         Acetone
        </a>
        is an important feeder material in the chemical industry.
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Oxygen
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Carbon
        </div>
        <div class="legend">
         <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid silver; background-color:white; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
         </span>
         Hydrogen
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ATP_structure.svg">
        <img alt="Skeletal chemical structure with a linear chain of O-P-O bonds connected to three different carbon-nitrogen rings." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="722" data-file-width="1236" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/ATP_structure.svg/220px-ATP_structure.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/ATP_structure.svg/330px-ATP_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/ATP_structure.svg/440px-ATP_structure.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ATP_structure.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Oxygen represents more than 40% of the
        <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">
         molecular mass
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">
         ATP
        </a>
        molecule.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Among the most important classes of organic compounds that contain oxygen are (where "R" is an organic group):
      <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">
       alcohols
      </a>
      (R-OH);
      <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">
       ethers
      </a>
      (R-O-R);
      <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">
       ketones
      </a>
      (R-CO-R);
      <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">
       aldehydes
      </a>
      (R-CO-H);
      <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">
       carboxylic acids
      </a>
      (R-COOH);
      <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">
       esters
      </a>
      (R-COO-R);
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid_anhydride" title="Acid anhydride">
       acid anhydrides
      </a>
      (R-CO-O-CO-R); and
      <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">
       amides
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       R-C(O)-NR
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ). There are many important organic
      <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">
       solvents
      </a>
      that contain oxygen, including:
      <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">
       acetone
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">
       methanol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">
       ethanol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Isopropyl_alcohol" title="Isopropyl alcohol">
       isopropanol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Furan" title="Furan">
       furan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">
       THF
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">
       diethyl ether
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/1,4-Dioxane" title="1,4-Dioxane">
       dioxane
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_acetate" title="Ethyl acetate">
       ethyl acetate
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dimethylformamide" title="Dimethylformamide">
       DMF
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">
       DMSO
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">
       acetic acid
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">
       formic acid
      </a>
      . Acetone (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       (CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       )
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       CO
      </span>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">
       phenol
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       C
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
       H
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        5
       </span>
       OH
      </span>
      ) are used as feeder materials in the synthesis of many different substances. Other important organic compounds that contain oxygen are:
      <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">
       glycerol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">
       formaldehyde
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Glutaraldehyde" title="Glutaraldehyde">
       glutaraldehyde
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">
       citric acid
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Acetic_anhydride" title="Acetic anhydride">
       acetic anhydride
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Acetamide" title="Acetamide">
       acetamide
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Epoxide" title="Epoxide">
       Epoxides
      </a>
      are ethers in which the oxygen atom is part of a ring of three atoms.
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen reacts spontaneously with many
      <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">
       organic
      </a>
      compounds at or below room temperature in a process called
      <a href="/wiki/Autoxidation" title="Autoxidation">
       autoxidation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE506_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE506-119">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of the
      <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
       organic compounds
      </a>
      that contain oxygen are not made by direct action of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      . Organic compounds important in industry and commerce that are made by direct oxidation of a precursor include
      <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">
       ethylene oxide
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Peracetic_acid" title="Peracetic acid">
       peracetic acid
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE507_116-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE507-116">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The element is found in almost all
      <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">
       biomolecules
      </a>
      that are important to (or generated by) life. Only a few common complex biomolecules, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">
       squalene
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Carotene" title="Carotene">
       carotenes
      </a>
      , contain no oxygen. Of the organic compounds with biological relevance,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">
       carbohydrates
      </a>
      contain the largest proportion by mass of oxygen. All
      <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">
       fats
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">
       fatty acids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">
       amino acids
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       proteins
      </a>
      contain oxygen (due to the presence of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">
       carbonyl
      </a>
      groups in these acids and their ester residues). Oxygen also occurs in
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">
       phosphate
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       PO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        3−
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      ) groups in the biologically important energy-carrying molecules
      <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">
       ATP
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_diphosphate" title="Adenosine diphosphate">
       ADP
      </a>
      , in the backbone and the
      <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">
       purines
      </a>
      (except
      <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">
       adenine
      </a>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">
       pyrimidines
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">
       RNA
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      , and in bones as
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_phosphate" title="Calcium phosphate">
       calcium phosphate
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydroxylapatite" title="Hydroxylapatite">
       hydroxylapatite
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_precautions">
       Safety and precautions
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">
       NFPA 704
      </a>
      standard rates compressed oxygen gas as nonhazardous to health, nonflammable and nonreactive, but an oxidizer. Refrigerated liquid oxygen (LOX) is given a health hazard rating of 3 (for increased risk of
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperoxia" title="Hyperoxia">
       hyperoxia
      </a>
      from condensed vapors, and for hazards common to cryogenic liquids such as frostbite), and all other ratings are the same as the compressed gas form.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">
       Toxicity
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_toxicity" title="Oxygen toxicity">
       Oxygen toxicity
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png">
        <img alt="A diagraph showing a man torso and listing symptoms of oxygen toxicity: Eyes – visual field loss, near)sightedness, cataract formation, bleeding, fibrosis; Head – seizures; Muscles – twitching; Respiratory system – jerky breathing, irritation, coughing, pain, shortness of breath, tracheobronchitis, acute respiratory distress syndrome." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1562" data-file-width="2067" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png/300px-Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png/450px-Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png/600px-Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_oxygen_toxicity.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Main symptoms of oxygen toxicity
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
         <a href="#cite_note-120">
          [111]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scuba-diving.jpg">
        <img alt="Four divers, equipped with oxygen cylinders, at the see bottom." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="360" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Scuba-diving.jpg/220px-Scuba-diving.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Scuba-diving.jpg/330px-Scuba-diving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Scuba-diving.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scuba-diving.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Oxygen toxicity occurs when the lungs take in
        <span class="nowrap">
        </span>
        <span class="frac nowrap">
         2
         <span class="visualhide">
         </span>
         <sup>
          1
         </sup>
         ⁄
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
        </span>
        higher than normal
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        partial pressure, which can occur in deep
        <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">
         scuba diving
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Oxygen gas (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ) can be
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_toxicity" title="Oxygen toxicity">
       toxic
      </a>
      at elevated
      <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">
       partial pressures
      </a>
      , leading to
      <a href="/wiki/Convulsion" title="Convulsion">
       convulsions
      </a>
      and other health problems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acott_104-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Acott-104">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        [j]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE511_122-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ECE511-122">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxygen toxicity usually begins to occur at partial pressures more than 50 kilo
      <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">
       pascals
      </a>
      (kPa), equal to about 50% oxygen composition at standard pressure or 2.5 times the normal sea-level
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      partial pressure of about 21 kPa. This is not a problem except for patients on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilator" title="Mechanical ventilator">
       mechanical ventilators
      </a>
      , since gas supplied through
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_mask" title="Oxygen mask">
       oxygen masks
      </a>
      in medical applications is typically composed of only 30%–50%
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      by volume (about 30 kPa at standard pressure).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB299_14-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB299-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (although this figure also is subject to wide variation, depending on type of mask).
     </p>
     <p>
      At one time,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_birth" title="Premature birth">
       premature babies
      </a>
      were placed in incubators containing
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      -rich air, but this practice was discontinued after some babies were blinded by the oxygen content being too high.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB299_14-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB299-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Breathing pure
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      in space applications, such as in some modern space suits, or in early spacecraft such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_spacecraft" title="Apollo spacecraft">
       Apollo
      </a>
      , causes no damage due to the low total pressures used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11541018_102-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11541018-102">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the case of spacesuits, the
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      partial pressure in the breathing gas is, in general, about 30 kPa (1.4 times normal), and the resulting
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      partial pressure in the astronaut's arterial blood is only marginally more than normal sea-level
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      partial pressure (for more information on this, see
      <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">
       space suit
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Arterial_blood_gas" title="Arterial blood gas">
       arterial blood gas
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen toxicity to the lungs and
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      can also occur in deep
      <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">
       scuba diving
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_supplied_diving" title="Surface supplied diving">
       surface supplied diving
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB299_14-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-NBB299-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acott_104-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Acott-104">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prolonged breathing of an air mixture with an
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      partial pressure more than 60 kPa can eventually lead to permanent
      <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_fibrosis" title="Pulmonary fibrosis">
       pulmonary fibrosis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BMJ-124">
        [114]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Exposure to a
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      partial pressures greater than 160 kPa (about 1.6 atm) may lead to convulsions (normally fatal for divers). Acute oxygen toxicity (causing seizures, its most feared effect for divers) can occur by breathing an air mixture with 21%
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      at 66 m (217 ft) or more of depth; the same thing can occur by breathing 100%
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      at only 6 m (20 ft).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ_124-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BMJ-124">
        [114]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald_125-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Donald-125">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald1_126-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Donald1-126">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald2_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Donald2-127">
        [117]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion_and_other_hazards">
       Combustion and other hazards
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_1_fire.jpg">
        <img alt="The inside of a small spaceship, charred and apparently destroyed." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2702" data-file-width="4000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Apollo_1_fire.jpg/220px-Apollo_1_fire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Apollo_1_fire.jpg/330px-Apollo_1_fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Apollo_1_fire.jpg/440px-Apollo_1_fire.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_1_fire.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The interior of the
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
         Apollo 1
        </a>
        Command Module. Pure
        <span class="chemf nowrap">
         O
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
        </span>
        at higher than normal pressure and a spark led to a fire and the loss of the
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
         Apollo 1
        </a>
        crew.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Highly concentrated sources of oxygen promote rapid combustion.
      <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">
       Fire
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">
       explosion
      </a>
      hazards exist when concentrated oxidants and
      <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">
       fuels
      </a>
      are brought into close proximity; an ignition event, such as heat or a spark, is needed to trigger combustion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss2008_31-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weiss2008-31">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astm-tpt_128-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-astm-tpt-128">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxygen is the oxidant, not the fuel, but nevertheless the source of most of the chemical energy released in combustion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss2008_31-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weiss2008-31">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt-Rohr2015_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-Rohr2015-39">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Combustion hazards also apply to compounds of oxygen with a high oxidative potential, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">
       peroxides
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chlorate" title="Chlorate">
       chlorates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">
       nitrates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">
       perchlorates
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dichromate" title="Dichromate">
       dichromates
      </a>
      because they can donate oxygen to a fire.
     </p>
     <p>
      Concentrated
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      will allow combustion to proceed rapidly and energetically.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astm-tpt_128-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-astm-tpt-128">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">
       Steel
      </a>
      pipes and storage vessels used to store and transmit both gaseous and
      <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">
       liquid oxygen
      </a>
      will act as a fuel; and therefore the design and manufacture of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      systems requires special training to ensure that ignition sources are minimized.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astm-tpt_128-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-astm-tpt-128">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The fire that killed the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
       Apollo 1
      </a>
      crew in a launch pad test spread so rapidly because the capsule was pressurized with pure
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      but at slightly more than atmospheric pressure, instead of the
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
      </span>
      normal pressure that would be used in a mission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        [k]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiles_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chiles-131">
        [120]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Liquid oxygen spills, if allowed to soak into organic matter, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">
       wood
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">
       petrochemicals
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">
       asphalt
      </a>
      can cause these materials to
      <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">
       detonate
      </a>
      unpredictably on subsequent mechanical impact.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astm-tpt_128-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-astm-tpt-128">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As with other
      <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">
       cryogenic
      </a>
      liquids, on contact with the human body it can cause
      <a href="/wiki/Frostbite" title="Frostbite">
       frostbites
      </a>
      to the skin and the eyes.
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-20">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-20">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         These results were mostly ignored until 1860. Part of this rejection was due to the belief that atoms of one element would have no
         <a href="/wiki/Chemical_affinity" title="Chemical affinity">
          chemical affinity
         </a>
         towards atoms of the same element, and part was due to apparent exceptions to Avogadro's law that were not explained until later in terms of dissociating molecules.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-30">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-30">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         An orbital is a concept from
         <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
          quantum mechanics
         </a>
         that models an electron as a
         <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave–particle duality">
          wave-like particle
         </a>
         that has a spatial distribution about an atom or molecule.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-33">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-33">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Oxygen's paramagnetism can be used analytically in paramagnetic oxygen gas analysers that determine the purity of gaseous oxygen. (
         <cite class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.servomex.com/oxygen_gas_analyser.html" rel="nofollow">
           "Company literature of Oxygen analyzers (triplet)"
          </a>
          . Servomex
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            December 15,
           </span>
           2007
          </span>
          .
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOxygen&amp;rft.btitle=Company+literature+of+Oxygen+analyzers+%28triplet%29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.servomex.com%2Foxygen_gas_analyser.html&amp;rft.pub=Servomex&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-61">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-61">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Figures given are for values up to 50 miles (80 km) above the surface
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-70">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-70">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Thylakoid membranes are part of
         <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">
          chloroplasts
         </a>
         in algae and plants while they simply are one of many membrane structures in cyanobacteria. In fact, chloroplasts are thought to have evolved from
         <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">
          cyanobacteria
         </a>
         that were once symbiotic partners with the progenerators of plants and algae.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-72">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-72">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Water oxidation is catalyzed by a
         <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">
          manganese
         </a>
         -containing
         <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">
          enzyme
         </a>
         complex known as the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolving_complex" title="Oxygen evolving complex">
          oxygen evolving complex
         </a>
         (OEC) or water-splitting complex found associated with the lumenal side of thylakoid membranes. Manganese is an important
         <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">
          cofactor
         </a>
         , and
         <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
          calcium
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">
          chloride
         </a>
         are also required for the reaction to occur. (Raven 2005)
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-78">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-78">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         (1.8 grams/min/person)×(60 min/h)×(24 h/day)×(365 days/year)×(6.6 billion people)/1,000,000 g/t=6.24 billion tonnes
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-106">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-106">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The reason is that increasing the proportion of oxygen in the breathing gas at low pressure acts to augment the inspired
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
         </span>
         partial pressure nearer to that found at sea-level.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-114">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-114">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Also, since oxygen has a higher electronegativity than hydrogen, the charge difference makes it a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">
          polar molecule
         </a>
         . The interactions between the different
         <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">
          dipoles
         </a>
         of each molecule cause a net attraction force.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-121">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-121">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Since
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
         </span>
         's partial pressure is the fraction of
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
         </span>
         times the total pressure, elevated partial pressures can occur either from high
         <span class="chemf nowrap">
          O
          <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
           <br/>
           2
          </span>
         </span>
         fraction in breathing gas or from high breathing gas pressure, or a combination of both.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-130">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-130">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         No single ignition source of the fire was conclusively identified, although some evidence points to arc from an electrical spark.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
          <a href="#cite_note-129">
           [119]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
      </ol>

        </i>
        . Oxford, England: Oxford University Press. pp. 297–304.
        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
         ISBN
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-19-850340-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-19-850340-7">
         0-19-850340-7
        </a>
        .
       </cite>
       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOxygen&amp;rft.atitle=Oxygen&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.aulast=Emsley&amp;rft.btitle=Nature%27s+Building+Blocks%3A+An+A-Z+Guide+to+the+Elements&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=0-19-850340-7&amp;rft.pages=297-304&amp;rft.place=Oxford%2C+England&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
        <span style="display:none;">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li>
       <cite class="citation book" id="Reference-idRaven2005">
        Raven, Peter H.; Evert, Ray F.; Eichhorn, Susan E. (2005).
        <i>
         Biology of Plants
        </i>
        (7th ed.). New York: W.H. Freeman and Company Publishers. pp. 115–27.
        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">

        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7167-1007-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-7167-1007-2">

        </a>
        .
       </cite>
       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOxygen&amp;rft.au=Eichhorn%2C+Susan+E.&amp;rft.au=Evert%2C+Ray+F.&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter+H.&amp;rft.aulast=Raven&amp;rft.btitle=Biology+of+Plants&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.edition=7th&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-7167-1007-2&amp;rft.pages=115-27&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=W.H.+Freeman+and+Company+Publishers&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
